It takes its origin from a lamp hanging over the threshold of a house which, by its peculiar <lbinfo n='po+sition'/> position, serves to light the rooms on both sides, and is used to denote something which serves a two-fold purpose at the same time. <P>15. рдиреГрдкрдирд╛рдкрд┐рддрдкреБрддреНрд░рдиреНрдпрд╛рдпрдГ The maxim of the king and barber's son. It is used to denote a man's innate <lbinfo n='fond+ness'/> fondness for his own <lbinfo n='possession-how+soever'/> possession-howsoever ugly or despicable in the eyes of others. It takes its origin from a story which states that a king on one occasion asked his barber to bring to him the finest boy that he could see in his kingdom. The barber roamed for a long time over every part of the realm, but could discover no boy such as the king wanted. At last wearied and disappointed, he returned home; and being charmed with the beauty of his own boy-who, to do him justice, was a personification of <lbinfo n='ug+liness'/> ugliness and deformity-went to the king and presented the boy to him. The king was at first very angry with the barber for having trifled with him, but on consideration excused him, as he ascribed the barber's <lbinfo n='pre+ference'/> preference of his own ugly boy to the dominant desire of human beings to consider their own possessions as supremely good; <ab>cf.</ab> -рд╕рд░реНрд╡рдГ рдХрд╛рдВрддрдорд╛рддреНрдореАрдпрдВ рдкрд╢реНрдпрддрд┐ <ls>┼Ъ. 2.</ls> <P>16. рдкрдВрдХрдкреНрд░рдХреНрд╖рд╛рд▓рдирдиреНрдпрд╛рдпрдГ The maxim of washing off the mud. Just as it is more advisable for one to avoid getting into mud than to get into it and then wash it off, so it is more advisable for one to avoid getting into danger than to expose oneself to it and then try to get out of it somehow or other; <ab>cf.</ab>  <lbinfo n='prakzAlanA#dDi'/> --рдкреНрд░рдХреНрд╖рд╛рд▓рдирд╛рджреНрдзрд┐ рдкрдВрдХрд╕реНрдп рджреВрд░рд╛рджрд╕реНрдкрд░реНрд╢рдирдВ рд╡рд░рдВ; and also <lbinfo n='тАЬPre+vention'/> тАЬPrevention is better than cureтАЭ. <P>17. рдкрд┐рд╖реНрдЯрдкреЗрд╖рдгрдиреНрдпрд╛рдпрдГ The maxim of grinding flour or meal, used to <lbinfo n='de+note'/> denote a superfluous or unprofitable exertion like the attempt of a man to grind pounded flour; <ab>cf.</ab> рдХреГрддрд╕реНрдп рдХрд░рдгрдВ рд╡реГрдерд╛. <P>18. рдмреАрдЬрд╛рдВрдХреБрд░рдиреНрдпрд╛рдпрдГ The maxim of seed and sprout. It takes its origin from the relation of mutual <lbinfo n='causa+tion'/> causation which subsists between seed and sprout, (<i>seed</i> being the cause of <i>sprout</i>, which in its turn is the cause of <i>seed</i>); and is used in those cases where two things stand to each other in the relation of both cause and effect. <P>19. рд▓реЛрд╣рдЪреБрдВрдмрдХрдиреНрдпрд╛рдпрдГ The maxim of iron and magnet; it is used to denote a very close affinity between two things, by virtue of which they are instinctively attracted towards each other, though at a distance. <P>20. рд╡рд╣реНрдирд┐рдзреВрдордиреНрдпрд╛рдпрдГ The maxim of the invariable concomitance of fire and smoke; (wherever there is smoke there is fire.). It is used to denote such invariable <lbinfo n='concomi+tance'/> concomitance between two persons or things; (<i><ab>e. g.</ab></i> where there is A, there is B; where there is not B, there is not A.). <P>21. рд╡рд┐рд╖рдХреГрдорд┐рдиреНрдпрд╛рдпрдГ The maxim of the worms bred in poison. It is used to denote a state of things which, though fatal to others, is not so to those who being bred in it, are inured or naturalized to it, like poison which, though fatal to others, is not so to the worms bred in it. <P>22. рд╡рд┐рд╖рд╡реГрдХреНрд╖рдиреНрдпрд╛рдпрдГ The maxim of the poison-tree; used to denote that a thing, though hurtful and mischievous, does not deserve to be destroyed by the very person who has reared it, just as even a <lbinfo n='poison+tree'/> poisontree ought not to be cut down by the planter himself. <P>23. рд╡реАрдЪрд┐рддрд░рдВрдЧрдиреНрдпрд╛рдпрдГ The maxim of a wave urging forward a wave. In the ocean one wave propels another till the first and all others in succession reach the shore. So this maxim is used to denote successive operation, as in the case of the <lbinfo n='pro+duction'/> production of sound. <P>24. рд╡реГрджреНрдзрдХреБрдорд╛рд░реАрд╡рд╛рдХреНрдп(рд╡рд░)рдиреНрдпрд╛рдпрдГ The maxim of the old virgin's boon; that is, asking such a boon as will cover all that one wishes to have. It is used when one casts a <lbinfo n='retro+spective'/> retrospective glance at what he has left behind, while at the same time he is proceeding, just as the lion, while going onward in search of prey, now and then bends his neck backwards to see if anything be within his reach; see under рд╕рд┐рдВрд╣ also. <P>27. рд╕реВрдЪреАрдХрдЯрд╛рд╣рдиреНрдпрд╛рдпрдГ The maxim of the needle and the kettle. It is used to denote that when two things-the one easy and the other difficult-are required to be done, the easier should be first attended to, as when one has to prepare a needle and a kettle, he should first take in hand a needle as it is an easier work compared with the <lbinfo n='pre+paration'/> preparation of a kettle. <P>28. рд╕реНрдерд╛рд▓реАрдкреБрд▓рд╛рдХрдиреНрдпрд╛рдпрдГ The maxim of the cooking-pot and boiled rice.
